  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_3e28baf5-7426-4e9c-98d6-b9275d872719'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

    };

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_2925725519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2925725519" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2925725519?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671702342" href="/book/show/112650.The_City_of_Marvels">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=historical-novel">historical-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=spanish-authors">spanish-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=barcelona">barcelona</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=worlds-fair">worlds-fair</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2925725519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7093965994553044493">City of Marvels by Eduardo Mendoza<br/><br/>A historical novel that is a love story to the authorâs hometown of Barcelona. The time frame is sandwiched between Barcelonaâs two Worldâs Fairs â 1888 and 1929. The main character is a poor boy from a rural area who is trying to escape poverty by coming to the city as a young teenager. He stays at a boarding house with a lot of characters (a fortune teller, a cross-dressing manager, a retired priest, a dentist/abortionist) and he gets a job distributing anarch</span>
  <span id="freeText7093965994553044493" style="display:none">City of Marvels by Eduardo Mendoza<br><br>A historical novel that is a love story to the authorâs hometown of Barcelona. The time frame is sandwiched between Barcelonaâs two Worldâs Fairs â 1888 and 1929. The main character is a poor boy from a rural area who is trying to escape poverty by coming to the city as a young teenager. He stays at a boarding house with a lot of characters (a fortune teller, a cross-dressing manager, a retired priest, a dentist/abortionist) and he gets a job distributing anarchist flyers. (Boarding house stories are always fun because of the diverse weird characters you get into --- I think of two other boarding house books I've read: The Boarding-House by William Trevor and Hotel Brasil: The Mystery of the Severed Heads by Frei Betto.)<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1565051454i/27944433._SX540_.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>He soon realizes there is no money in flyers so he starts selling hair tonic on the side. His entrepreneurial skills attract the attention of the âmobâ so to speak, a group of politically-connected corrupt men making money off of the cityâs expenditures for the 1888 Fair and stealing expensive equipment from the exhibits. <br><br>He becomes an amoral man. Even as a young boy he forces a hotel maid to have sex with him so he will help her father (the cross-dresser) get out of prison. The main character becomes not only corrupt but even hires a hit man to take people out. Itâs like Chicago in the 1930âs. There are gang wars among rival groups and eventually the still-young man rises to the top. He comes to believe that âSociety rests on these four pillarsâ¦ignorance, negligence, injustice, and folly.â We follow his life and loves as time goes on. <br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1565051454i/27944434._SX540_.jpg" width="363" height="242" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The main character moves on from local corruption and at various times gets involved in land speculation, diamond and weapons smuggling. He becomes an entrepreneur in Barcelonaâs start-up movie industry, picking out women and making them stars, although this is one endeavor on which he loses money. Still, he becomes the richest man in all of Spain but never really helps his poverty-stricken parents back on the farm. <br><br>While the main character is fictional, real people and true events are mixed in with the story, such as Antoni Gaudi, architect of the Sagrada Familia and Rasputin, and factual incidents about the scope and development of the worldâs fairs. To an extent, the book is a fairly factual history of the urban development of Barcelona. <br><br>Itâs a good story and it kept my attention but there are passages that should have been edited out. For example, there are long lists of the names of engineers who worked on the fair (whether real or fictional, what is the point of that in a novel?). There are overly-statistical passages on the capacity of water displays and electricity needs. Still, overall, a good read. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1565051829i/27944444._SY540_.jpg" width="363" height="442" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The author (1943-) has written a dozen-or-so novels of which this one is probably his best, according to Wiki and Goodreads. <br><br>Top two photos from atlasobscura.com<br><br>Photo of the author from Wikipedia <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="7093965994553044493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151180400" href="/book/show/3172904-the-city-of-marvels">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2422276-mag?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2422276-mag?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2422276-mag?shelf=satire">satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2422276-mag?shelf=barcelona">barcelona</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127562819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9060772524769768796">I have a feeling that Mendoza is better known in Europe than in North America. I have already read four of his books, none of which has been published here I donât think. The three were on a lighter side with an insane barber turned detective as the main protagonist- sort of Being There by Kosinski but on a very light side.<br/>Even though all them are set in Barcelona, this one, The City of Marvels, is about Barcelona itself. Yes, it does have a plot that kept me reading even though my interest pete</span>
  <span id="freeText9060772524769768796" style="display:none">I have a feeling that Mendoza is better known in Europe than in North America. I have already read four of his books, none of which has been published here I donât think. The three were on a lighter side with an insane barber turned detective as the main protagonist- sort of Being There by Kosinski but on a very light side.<br />Even though all them are set in Barcelona, this one, The City of Marvels, is about Barcelona itself. Yes, it does have a plot that kept me reading even though my interest petered out by the end, but the main focus is on historical Barcelona. The times that are of special interest to the author are of and between the two International Expositions which took place there in 1888 and 1929 respectively. The main character, Onofre, is a peasant who comes to the big city with a plan to get rich and through his intelligence and cunning, crime and lack of scruples becomes one of the richest men in Barcelona and Spain. Were I not acquainted with Mendoza, I would probably not know what to make of this main character. He is definitely not a likeable person and for us to like him is not the point. The point Mendoza is making is that this was the way people got rich in Barcelona during that time and this was the way Barcelona acquired its bourgeoisie. That may not be entirely true either, since the whole thing is a satire and I feel that we are to read it as such. All in all itâs the story of the city Mendoza is dying to tell here; Onofre is only an excuse.<br /><br />3.5/5</span>
  <a data-text-id="9060772524769768796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15825490278385249239">It has been said before: the book is more about the city of Barcelona than about the person Onofre Bouvila. That isn't the biggest issue however. On lots of occasions I missed depth, spots where the plot really could have been more intense. Missed chances, even. I couldn't help associating parts of the story with Zafons Shadow of the Wind series, which took all of these possibilities and added more depth and more magic to it.<br/></span>

<div id="review_469879839" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/469879839" />
    <a title="Dwight" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight"><img alt="Dwight" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1314134316p2/1393281.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/469879839?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dwight" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dwight" href="/user/show/1393281-dwight">Dwight</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671702342" href="/book/show/112650.The_City_of_Marvels">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer469879839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2968138495823557328"><a href='http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/07/city-of-marvels-by-eduardo-mendoza.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>My comments on the book</a><br/><br/>From the bookflap:<br/> <blockquote>Here is the story of Onofre Bouvila, a poor Catalan country boy who arrives in Barcelona in 1886 and lies, cheats, and murders his way to enormous wealth and power.<br/><br/> Young Onofre first stays at a boardinghouse whose permanent residents are a distingue closet transvestite, a lady fortuneteller who treats her clients to large doses of doom, a garrulous barber who also pulls teeth, and a scullery maid who protects her virginity with the aid of a ferocious ca</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText2968138495823557328" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/07/city-of-marvels-by-eduardo-mendoza.html" rel="nofollow noopener">My comments on the book</a><br /><br />From the bookflap:<br /> <blockquote>Here is the story of Onofre Bouvila, a poor Catalan country boy who arrives in Barcelona in 1886 and lies, cheats, and murders his way to enormous wealth and power.<br /><br /> Young Onofre first stays at a boardinghouse whose permanent residents are a distingue closet transvestite, a lady fortuneteller who treats her clients to large doses of doom, a garrulous barber who also pulls teeth, and a scullery maid who protects her virginity with the aid of a ferocious cat. Onofre, for his part, quickly establishes himself as an anarchist agitator. Yet he realizes just as quickly that his skills as organizer can be put to better useâselling a cure for baldness. He goes on to form a burglary ring, instigate real estate deals, build a film empire, but a world-famous diamond, reconstruct a mansion with eleven balconies, fall in and out of love with three women, and throw a reception for the Tsarina of Russiaâwhere he chats with Rasputin while planning to sell arms to the Bolsheviks.<br /><br /> Meanwhile, in marvelous digressions, we learn about an old sailor who speaks a language no academic can identify; a nun who plays the accordion for the dying to cheer them up; a missionary priest who goes to the Sudan to make converts but is himself converted to the Dervish religion and returns home to preach sorcery; and about Eulalia, the patron saint of Barcelona, who steps down from her pedestal in the cathedral to talk to the mayor about housing problems.</blockquote><br /><br />I find writing about <i>The City of Marvels</i> difficult because I had little response to it. There are parts I enjoyed, usually with some of the digressions mentioned above, while most of the book didnât move me to either like or hate it. Which is a shame because the book is very well doneâI respect Mendozaâs talent even when reading through parts I find problematic.<br /><br />I included the bookflap summary because it does a fair job of capturing the general flow of the book, which ultimately is about the development of Barcelona between the World Fairs hosted in the city in 1888 and 1929. Weaving history, statistics, lore, and fabrication into the narrative, Mendoza captures the era and the cityâs struggle to modernize, oftentimes in spite of itself. Also reflected is the increasing tension between Madrid and Catalonia. To some extent the character of Onofre Bouvila reflects these struggles, first buffeted by the changes and later shaping them. Onofreâs disgust with his father, a bankrupt and scammer after his time spent in Cuba, echoes the general feeling of disappointment with the venal government. In order to succeed in this atmosphere Onofre has to exceed in cruelty and corruption, even when his intentions are good.<br /><br />Every now and then the book comes alive, such as when Mendoza goes into detail about the gang war that established Onofre as the power behind a shadowy throne or when weaving a tale about Mata Hariâs capture that involves denying Spain receipt of the world's first full-length feature film. Too often, though, these scenes highlight how flat other parts feel. Despite spending a lot of time on Onofre, the character rarely seems to develop or grow, although now that I think about it, for someone so closely tied to the city that he supposedly embodies the spirit of the era and the cityâs collective dreams, that may be precisely the point.<br /><br />Similar to my comment in the post on <i>The Truth about the Savolta Case</i>, Mendoza seems to be anticipating the future while looking at the past. Just as the structure of the book uses the bookends of the two World Fairs to look at Barcelonaâs entrance to the modern world, there seems to be an implied question as to Barcelonaâs future. This goes beyond the then-upcoming 1992 Olympics the city would host (the book was released in 1986) and reflects on the cost, not limited to monetary measures, associated with such progress.<br /><br />If youâve read the book, Iâm very interested in hearing your comments on it. And, of course, since there was a movie based on the book, Iâll be watching that and posting on it later this week (hopefully).<br /><br />Update: I've added a post on the <a href="http://bookcents.blogspot.com/2012/07/la-ciudad-de-los-prodigios-city-of.html" rel="nofollow noopener">movie adaptation of the novel</a>. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2968138495823557328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10979838650280199118">I was a bit disappointed with this one. I have read a few other novels by Mendoza, which I liked best.<br/><br/>Punctuation is weird (different sentences chained together with commas are less legible). The author rarely uses quotation marks or quotation dashes, so it't up to the reader to guess where some character stops speaking, and where the narrator resumes narrating. Full stops are scarce, and Mendoza mixes different situations and times in the same paragraphâ¦<br/><br/>The story itself is entertaining, funny a</span>
  <span id="freeText10979838650280199118" style="display:none">I was a bit disappointed with this one. I have read a few other novels by Mendoza, which I liked best.<br /><br />Punctuation is weird (different sentences chained together with commas are less legible). The author rarely uses quotation marks or quotation dashes, so it't up to the reader to guess where some character stops speaking, and where the narrator resumes narrating. Full stops are scarce, and Mendoza mixes different situations and times in the same paragraphâ¦<br /><br />The story itself is entertaining, funny at times. But I felt frustrated every time an actual Historical figure or event was mentioned and Mendoza made up quite a lot of that, adding weird developments to what happened in reality.<br /><br />Characters aren't well defined. The personality of the main character alone is an amorphous mix of a rascal, a businessman and a thug. It is not clear what are his feelings, or his feelings seem to change whimsically.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186940034" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6629249459494789058">Written in Spanish in 1986 ( La Cuidad de los Prodigios)and translated into English in 1988 by Bernard Molloy, THE CITY OF MARVELS is a chronicle of the modern age in which historical facts are combined with the story of the rise to power of Onofre Bouvila, a scheming, self-serving peasant boy who comes to Barcelona in search of work.<br/>Set in the time period between 1888 and 1929, Mendoza's vivid portrayal of Barcelona, in all its splendor and squalor, echoes Onofre's rapid ascent to power and fab</span>
  <span id="freeText6629249459494789058" style="display:none">Written in Spanish in 1986 ( La Cuidad de los Prodigios)and translated into English in 1988 by Bernard Molloy, THE CITY OF MARVELS is a chronicle of the modern age in which historical facts are combined with the story of the rise to power of Onofre Bouvila, a scheming, self-serving peasant boy who comes to Barcelona in search of work.<br />Set in the time period between 1888 and 1929, Mendoza's vivid portrayal of Barcelona, in all its splendor and squalor, echoes Onofre's rapid ascent to power and fabulous riches; and can be seen as symbolic of the rise of the Barcelona bourgeoisie. <br />With its multitude of eccentric characters, sardonic asides, and bizarre incidents, THE CITY OF MARVELS is hugely entertaining---a very good read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6629249459494789058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer624721889" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16874580834134633438">Recommended by a friend, it was a bit of a struggle to get this 1986 book. The book reads more like a fairy tale, where a slightly absurd take on history is intertwined with the archetypical but rather flat endeavours of a country boy trying to make it in the big city, being saved by a deus ex machina on a few occasions. Then there's the thing that the book doesn't seem to be able to decide whether it's going to tell the history of its leading character or the history of the city of Barcelona. <br/><br/>M</span>
  <span id="freeText16874580834134633438" style="display:none">Recommended by a friend, it was a bit of a struggle to get this 1986 book. The book reads more like a fairy tale, where a slightly absurd take on history is intertwined with the archetypical but rather flat endeavours of a country boy trying to make it in the big city, being saved by a deus ex machina on a few occasions. Then there's the thing that the book doesn't seem to be able to decide whether it's going to tell the history of its leading character or the history of the city of Barcelona. <br /><br />Mendoza's book is more like a collection of snap shots over time that together loosely tell a story, both of the city and of the main character. But although the many side stories make the town's story reasonably interesting, the character's story suffers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16874580834134633438" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer713601678" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16269574513836967804">A big book which started off quite slow... once it got into the main story I enjoyed it and felt it was a great way of learning about the city of Barcelona during the period (1180's -1920's - ish).<br/>By the final third it was jumping all over the place - passages of past events mixed in the middle of current events - I found that confusing and annoying. It then felt like it was trying too hard - to fit in events and occurrences of the period rather than continuing a story that everything fit with. </span>
  <span id="freeText16269574513836967804" style="display:none">A big book which started off quite slow... once it got into the main story I enjoyed it and felt it was a great way of learning about the city of Barcelona during the period (1180's -1920's - ish).<br />By the final third it was jumping all over the place - passages of past events mixed in the middle of current events - I found that confusing and annoying. It then felt like it was trying too hard - to fit in events and occurrences of the period rather than continuing a story that everything fit with. I'm left wondering what did actually happen and what was fictional.</span>

<div id="review_1940417563" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1940417563" />
    <a title="Francisco Presas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62479589-francisco-presas"><img alt="Francisco Presas" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Francisco Presas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Francisco Presas" href="/user/show/62479589-francisco-presas">Francisco Presas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1940417563" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15166877883896802795">Between the magic realism and the surrealism. Mendoza fresh and cultivated style takes us to the Barcelona nineteenth century. We follow the development of the city and get engaged by the colorful collage of characters. No moral is intended, just showing the lights and shadows of the end of that century in the city.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2483246959" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2483246959" />
    <a title="Ingo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67998518-ingo"><img alt="Ingo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1496681530p2/67998518.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2483246959?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ingo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ingo" href="/user/show/67998518-ingo">Ingo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2483246959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer974763715972953460">The book changes genres, sometimes loses focus, often not convincing<br/><br/>The book starts out in a yarn telling style about the beginnings of Barcelona and its rise o a European Metropolis from the 1880s onward. We follow young Onofre Bouvilla to guide us through the different parts of the city, the different kind of people, who live there, from the 1st world exposition in 1888 to the second one in 1925.<br/>The first part of the book recounts a lot of anecdotes surrounding the cityÂ´s early days always in </span>
  <span id="freeText974763715972953460" style="display:none">The book changes genres, sometimes loses focus, often not convincing<br /><br />The book starts out in a yarn telling style about the beginnings of Barcelona and its rise o a European Metropolis from the 1880s onward. We follow young Onofre Bouvilla to guide us through the different parts of the city, the different kind of people, who live there, from the 1st world exposition in 1888 to the second one in 1925.<br />The first part of the book recounts a lot of anecdotes surrounding the cityÂ´s early days always in an ingenious funny way that makes very good entertainment. The characters are often caricatured, like those in the Pension Braulio, where a fortune-teller, a priest, an anarchist maiden and the innkeeper himself, who likes to go out at night, dressed like a woman, all live under the same roof. Onoufre falls in love with the anarchist maiden, but her fearsome cat impedes him to approach her (That doesn't really do it for me). In this part Onoufre makes his first bucks selling fake hair growth elixir with the help of a friend.<br />In this part also the fortune-teller predicts the course of his life, which will be mainly determined by three women (in my opinion the fortune-teller is plot device, that is a bit outworn, but...)<br />Onoufre is very successful with this shtick, so it is no surprise he gets the attention of a underworld boss who makes him work for him. Here the tone of the story changes and it gets very bloody. We now have a story of the rise of a gangster in BarcelonaÂ´s. Soon he has made himself indispensable to the underworld boss by disposing his rivals and finally he marries his daughter. Meanwhile, the first world exposition has taken place, the workers were on strike and rebelled and some other stuff happened in the city.<br />Now jumping into the 3rd part Onoufre finds himself to be the richest and most powerful man in Spain. Unfortunately the story loses track, and we see Onoufre trying to reconnect with his past in a small village in Catalonia, in brothels in the shady Raval quarter, he is lost in regret and reminiscence, since he sacrificed many friends for his personal gain. He detests his wife and children who he treats with cruelty and ends up buying a run down villa of a noble family which he now starts to rebuild accurate to the original in every detail. This part of the story was a very hard and boring read to me. Meanwhile, Primo de Reibera is in power, SpainÂ´s first dictator, and Onoufre is very much enmeshed with high society and big money, although they treat him like second class.<br />In the last part the third woman in OnoufreÂ´s life shows up- so the fortune-teller was right !<br />It is the barely 18 y.o. daughter of an inventor we have met before, 100 pages or so, when she was just a little girl. She falls in love with Onoufre, now in his fifties - no questions asked.<br />This last part deals with cultural (film) and technical progress (aviation) and goes back o the hilarious yarn telling we saw in the beginning (those are the best parts of the book in my opinion).<br /><br />To sum it up I was a bit disappointed because the plot did not convince me much, and for me Onoufre stays a bit flat at times as a character. His phase of introspective and regret in the third part feel somewhat second hand to me. The fortune-tellerÂ´s prediction, which gives structure to the story, were not followed up much. As soon as married, the wife stays off scene throughout the whole book, for example.<br /><br />Interesting side note: another Barcelona author (who writes about Barcelona) is Ruiz ZafÃ³n. In his series starting with "La Sombra del viento" et al. you can find so many plot devices, even characters and ideas that seem to come directly from MendozaÂ´s work. Only, Ruiz ZafÃ³n did it better :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="974763715972953460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Plentiful, as expected, of mistery, plot twists, and his trademark sense of humor - often dark and raw, yet expressed with such elegance of words, that one would find difficult to feel offended by it. It won't disappoint his fans.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2094845239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13562383662224603269">La Ciudad de los Prodigios transports you to a fictional late XIX and early XX century Barcelona. It feels so real that I frequently checked online for historical facts, only to realise then that most were only the result of Eduardo Mendoza's imagination. It is an intricate story going through several generations and social changes. An excellent read, strongly recommended.</span>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Y059EPrtAboKEoEcDWOZw16SKzWSnn3P2WR8J/F5GNu9xbM3CYxs1DRsQ7XpiiHO5t69HrwKyyNfAcv0jMn+Yg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/37020.Eduardo_Mendoza">About Eduardo Mendoza</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/37020.Eduardo_Mendoza"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1249742380p3/37020.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/37020.Eduardo_Mendoza">Eduardo Mendoza
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15530025493141488211">Eduardo Mendoza studied law in the first half of the 1960s and lived in New York between 1973 and 1982, working as interpreter for the United Nations.<br/><br/>He maintained an intense relationship with novelists Juan Benet and Juan GarcÃ­a Hortelano, poet Pere Gimferrer and writer (and neighbour) FÃ©lix de AzÃºa.<br/><br/>In 1975 he published his very successful first novel, <i>La verdad sobre el caso Savolta (The Truth a</i></span>
  <span id="freeText15530025493141488211" style="display:none">Eduardo Mendoza studied law in the first half of the 1960s and lived in New York between 1973 and 1982, working as interpreter for the United Nations.<br /><br />He maintained an intense relationship with novelists Juan Benet and Juan GarcÃ­a Hortelano, poet Pere Gimferrer and writer (and neighbour) FÃ©lix de AzÃºa.<br /><br />In 1975 he published his very successful first novel, <i>La verdad sobre el caso Savolta (The Truth about the Savolta Case)</i>, where he shows his ability to use different resources and styles. The novel is considered a precursor to the social change in the Spanish post-Franco society and the first novel of the transition to democracy. He describes the union fights from the beginning of the 20th century, showing the social, cultural and economic reality of the Barcelona at the time. A year later he was awarded the Critic Prize.<br /><br />His most acclaimed novel is probably <i>La ciudad de los prodigios (The City of Marvels</i>, 1986), about the social and urban evolution of Barcelona between the Universal Expositions of 1888 and 1929. It was adapted to the screen by Mario Camus in 1999.<br /><br />In 1996, he published his third major Barcelona novel, this time set in the 1940s, <i>Una comedia ligera (A Light Comedy)</i>.<br /><br />Also within Mendoza's work stands the saga of Ceferino, a peculiar character, a detective locked up in a mental hospital. The first of these novels, <i>El misterio de la cripta embrujada (The Mystery of the Bewitched Crypt</i>, 1979) is a parody with hilarious moments mixing detective stories with gothic narrative.<br /><br />In the second novel of the saga, <i>El laberinto de las aceitunas (The Labyrinth of the Olives</i>, 1982) he confirms his talent as parodist; the novel is one of his most successful works. The third (and last) novel of the saga, <i>La aventura del tocador de seÃ±oras (The Adventure of the Powder Room)</i> was published in 2002.<br /><br />The newspaper <i>El PaÃ­s</i> published two of his novels by instalments, <i>Sin noticias de Gurb ( No Word from Gurb</i>, 1990) and <i>El Ãºltimo trayecto de Horacio Dos (The Last Journey of Horatio Dos</i>, 2001).<br /><br />In 1990, his work in Catalan <i>RestauraciÃ³</i> made its debut. He later translated it into Spanish himself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15530025493141488211" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
